Now, note that the sequence a_n satisfies a_{n+1}=f(a_n). If a_n is a fixed point, then the sequence becomes constant from that point onward. So we need to check all starting numbers a1 (with at least three proper divisors) and see if the process can continue indefinitely.

We observed that for many numbers, the process quickly leads to a number with fewer than three proper divisors (like 7, 19, etc.). So it seems that the fixed points are exactly numbers N such that N is divisible by 6, but not by 4 or 5. So these are numbers of the form N = 6 * m where m is odd and not divisible by 5. For these N, f(N)=N. So they are valid a1.

Now, we also have numbers that are not fixed points but eventually reach a fixed point, like 72. So what is the condition for a number to eventually reach a fixed point without hitting a dead end? For a1=72, we have f(72)=78, which is fixed. So 72 is a preimage of a fixed point. But we need to ensure that f(72) itself has at least three proper divisors (which it does, since 78 has three proper divisors). So 72 is valid. Similarly, we might have numbers that map to 72, etc. So the set of all valid a1 is the set of numbers that are in the "forward orbit" of a fixed point, but also we must consider that during iteration, we never hit a number with <3 proper divisors. That means that all numbers in the orbit must have at least three proper divisors. Since the fixed points themselves have at least three proper divisors (they have divisors 1,2,3,6,... actually they have at least 1,2,3,6, so proper divisors: at least 1,2,3,6? For N=6, proper:1,2,3; for N=18, proper:1,2,3,6,9; etc. So they are fine). So if a number eventually reaches a fixed point, then after that it's constant, so infinite sequence. So the only potential problem is if during the iteration before reaching the fixed point, we encounter a number that does not have three proper divisors. So we need to examine the forward orbit of each candidate a1.

Thus, the problem reduces to: Determine all positive integers a1 such that the recursion a_{n+1}=f(a_n) can be defined for all n (i.e., each a_n has at least three proper divisors). Since f is defined for numbers with at least three proper divisors, we need to ensure that a1 itself has at least three proper divisors (given). Then a2=f(a1) must also have at least three proper divisors; otherwise the process stops. So we need to find all a1 such that all iterates have at least three proper divisors.

We can try to characterize the behavior of f. Note that f(N) is always less than N? Not always; as we saw, for N=30, f=31>30; for N=42, f=42; for N=18, f=18; for N=12, f=13>12; for N=20, f=19<20; for N=24, f=26>24; for N=36, f=39>36; for N=48, f=52>48; for N=54, f=54; for N=66, f=66; for N=72, f=78>72; for N=84, f=91>84; for N=90, f=93>90; for N=96, f=104>96; for N=108, f=117>108; for N=120, f=130>120; for N=132, f=143>132; for N=144, f=156>144; for N=150, f=155>150; for N=168, f=182>168; for N=180, f=195>180; for N=186, f=186. So many numbers increase, but some decrease. For numbers that increase, they might increase indefinitely or eventually hit a fixed point? Thus, the valid a1 are those numbers that, when iterated, eventually reach a fixed point without ever hitting a number with fewer than three proper divisors. Since fixed points are infinite, the set of valid a1 might be infinite as well, but maybe there is a characterization.

Observation: If a number N is even and not divisible by 4, then N = 2 * odd. The smallest divisors: 2, then the next smallest divisor is the smallest odd prime factor? Now, numbers that are not fixed points but map to a fixed point: For N=72, N=72=2^3*3^2, exponent of 2 is 3, so divisible by 4. Its f(72)=78, which is a fixed point. So 72 works. Then those N are also valid, because then a2=72, and then a3=78, etc. So we need to consider the whole tree of preimages of fixed points, and also numbers that may map to a number that eventually reaches a fixed point, etc. So the set of valid a1 is the set of numbers that eventually reach a fixed point under iteration of f, provided that all iterates have at least three proper divisors. But note: If a number maps to a number that is not a fixed point, but that number eventually reaches a fixed point, that's fine as long as the intermediate numbers also have at least three proper divisors. So the valid a1 are exactly the numbers that are in the "forward orbit" of a fixed point, i.e., numbers that eventually iterate to a fixed point. Since the recursion is deterministic, we can try to characterize the set of numbers that do not lead to a dead end.

Observation: The recursion tends to decrease numbers? Actually, from the examples, many numbers increase until they hit a number that is either prime or has <3 proper divisors. But some numbers like 72 increase to 78 (fixed). Others increase to a prime like 30->31. So the process can be seen as: Starting from a1, we apply f repeatedly. If at any step we get a number that is either prime or has only two proper divisors, the sequence terminates. So we need to avoid that.

Thus, we need to find all N such that f(N) is defined and also f(N) has at least three proper divisors, and then continue. This is a kind of "survival" condition.

Given the complexity, perhaps there is a known result or pattern. Let's analyze f more deeply.

Let N be a positive integer with at least three proper divisors. Write N's prime factorization. Let p be the smallest prime factor. Then N/p is the largest proper divisor. Let q be the next smallest prime factor? Not exactly; we need the three smallest divisors >1.

Define d1 = smallest prime factor = p.
Define d2 = smallest divisor >1 that is not equal to p, i.e., min(p^2 if p^2|N, else next prime factor q).
Define d3 = next smallest divisor after d2.

Then f(N) = N*(1/d1+1/d2+1/d3) = N*(1/p+1/d2+1/d3).

Now, note that d1,d2,d3 are all divisors of N, and they are all at most N. Also, note that f(N) is always an integer because N/d1,N/d2,N/d3 are integers.

We can consider the case p=2 separately. For even N, p=2. Then f(N)=N*(1/2+1/d2+1/d3). For N odd, p>=3.

From these examples, we see that the numbers that work are exactly those that are multiples of 6 and also satisfy that N/2 is odd (i.e., exponent of 2 is 1) and N not divisible by 5. But we also have 72 works, which is divisible by 4, so exponent of 2 is 3, not 1. So that condition is not necessary for all valid a1. However, 72 is a preimage of a fixed point. So the set of valid a1 includes all numbers that eventually map to a fixed point. So we need to find all numbers that are in the forward orbit of a fixed point (i.e., numbers that eventually reach a fixed point). But note: If a number maps to a number that is not a fixed point but eventually reaches a fixed point, that's fine. So the set of all numbers that do not lead to a dead end is the set of numbers that are in the "basin of attraction" of the fixed points. Since the fixed points are numbers of the form 6k where k is odd and not divisible by 5, these are all multiples of 6 with no factor 4 and no factor 5. So the basin includes all numbers that, under iteration, eventually land in that set.

Thus, we need to characterize all N such that iterating f eventually yields a number N' with N' = 6 * m, m odd and not divisible by 5. That is, N' is a fixed point.

But note: The process might also lead to a fixed point that is not of that form? We determined that all fixed points are exactly those numbers. So we need to find all N that eventually iterate to a number of that form.

725->199 dead. 727 prime. 729->351 dead. So far, all odd numbers we tested eventually lead to a prime or a number with exactly two proper divisors (like 25, 49, etc.). It seems plausible that all odd numbers >1 eventually iterate to a prime or to a number with only two proper divisors. But we need to prove that the only possible infinite sequences are those that eventually become constant at a fixed point, which are even numbers with no factor 4 and no factor 5. And also we must consider that numbers that map to these fixed points are valid, but we must ensure that during the iteration, all numbers have at least three proper divisors. For instance, 72 works because its f is 78 (fixed point), and 78 has three proper divisors. But what about numbers that map to 72? For instance, is there any N such that f(N)=72? Let's solve f(N)=72. We need to find all N with at least three proper divisors and sum of three largest proper divisors =72. Let's try to find preimages. For even N, if N is even and not divisible by 4, then d1=2, d2 is smallest odd divisor, d3 = min(next odd divisor, 2*d2). Then f(N)=N*(1/2+1/d2+1/d3). Since N=2*m, f(N)=m*(1+1/d2+1/d3)? Actually, N/2=m, so f(N)=m*(1+1/d2+1/d3)? Let's derive: f(N)=N*(1/2+1/d2+1/d3)=2m*(1/2+1/d2+1/d3)=m*(1+2/d2+2/d3). That might be messy. Alternatively, we can directly compute possible N. Since f(N)=72, and f(N) is always at least something? For N with at least three proper divisors, f(N) is typically less than N for odd N, and for even N it can be greater or equal. But we can try to find all N such that f(N)=72.

Let's try to find small N that map to 72. Compute f(N) for N=... We already have f(72)=78, f(78)=78, so 72 maps to 78, not 72. So 72 is not a fixed point. So f(N)=72 would mean N is a preimage of 72. Let's compute f(N) for some N near 72. f(70)=59, f(66)=66, f(68)=? 68=2^2*17, divisors:1,2,4,17,34,68. Three largest:34,17,4 sum=55. f(68)=55. f(64)=56, f(60)=65, f(52)=43, f(48)=52, f(36)=39, f(42)=42, f(54)=54, f(72)=78, f(84)=91, f(90)=93, f(96)=104, f(100)=95, f(102)=102, f(108)=117, f(114)=114, f(120)=130, f(126)=126, f(132)=143, f(144)=156, f(150)=155, f(156)=169, f(162)=162, f(168)=182, f(174)=174, f(180)=195, f(186)=186, f(192)=? 192=2^6*3, divisors: many. Largest:96,48,32 sum=176. f(192)=176. So 72 doesn't appear as f(N) from these. But maybe there is some N with f(N)=72. Let's solve f(N)=72. Write N's prime factorization. Let p be smallest prime factor. Then f(N)=N*(1/p+1/d2+1/d3)=72. Since N>1, and 1/p+1/d2+1/d3 is a rational number. For even N, p=2. Then f(N)=N*(1/2+1/d2+1/d3)=72 => N=144/(1+2/d2+2/d3). Since N integer, 1+2/d2+2/d3 must divide 144. Also d2,d3 are divisors >1, with d2<d3. Also d2 is the second smallest divisor, d3 is the third smallest. Typically d2 is either 3,4,5,... depending on N. We can try to find possible (d2,d3) such that N=144/(1+2/d2+2/d3) is an integer and N has the given d2,d3 as its smallest divisors. This is a bit involved.

But we don't necessarily need to find all preimages; we need to determine all possible a1 that lead to an infinite sequence. So we need to characterize all N such that the sequence never hits a number with <3 proper divisors. This is reminiscent of a known problem: "A proper divisor of a positive integer N is a positive divisor of N other than N itself. The infinite sequence a1,a2,... consists of positive integers, each of which has at least three proper divisors. For each n>=1, the integer a_{n+1} is the sum of the three largest proper divisors of a_n. Determine all possible values of a1." I think I've seen a similar problem. The answer might be that a1 must be a multiple of 6 and also not divisible by any prime p such that p<6? Actually, we already have many fixed points: numbers that are multiples of 6 with no factor 4 and no factor 5. But also numbers like 72 work, which is divisible by 4. So the condition is not just that a1 is a fixed point or preimage. But we need to see if there are any a1 that are not eventually fixed but still infinite. For instance, could there be a cycle that includes even numbers that are not fixed? Let's test some numbers that are multiples of 6 but not fixed: 30,36,48,60,72,84,90,96,108,120,132,144,150,156,168,180,192, etc. We already computed some: 30->31 dead, 36->39->17 dead, 48->52->43 dead, 60->65 dead, 72->78 fixed, 84->91 dead, 90->93 dead, 96->104->91 dead, 108->117->61 dead, 120->130->104->91 dead, 132->143->25 dead, 144->156->169 dead, 150->155->37 dead, 156->169 dead, 168->182->131 dead, 180->195->119->25 dead, 192->176->161->... dead, 198 fixed, 204? 204=2^2*3*17, divisors:1,2,3,4,6,12,17,34,51,68,102,204. Three largest:102,68,51 sum=221? Actually 102+68+51=221, so f(204)=221, then 221=13*17, f=17+13+1=31 dead. So fails. 210=2*3*5*7, f=105+42+30=177? Actually largest:105, then 42, then 30? Divisors:1,2,3,5,6,7,10,14,15,21,30,35,42,70,105,210. Three largest:105,70,42 sum=217, then 217=7*31, f=31+7+1=39 dead. So fails. 216=2^3*3^3, divisors: many. Largest:108, then 72, then 54? Actually divisors:1,2,3,4,6,8,9,12,18,24,27,36,54,72,108,216. Three largest:108,72,54 sum=234, then 234=2*3^2*13, f=117+78+39=234? 117+78+39=234, so 234 is a fixed point? Check: 234=6*39, but 39 is 3*13, so 234 is divisible by 2 and 3 and also by 4? 234/2=117 odd, so exponent of 2 is 1; 234 mod 5 = 4, so not divisible by 5; so it's a fixed point. So 216->234 fixed. So 216 works. So many numbers map to fixed points.

Thus, the set of valid a1 is the set of numbers that, under iteration, eventually reach a fixed point (i.e., a number of the form 6k with k odd and not divisible by 5). But note: The iteration is defined only if each a_n has at least three proper divisors. So if a number maps to a fixed point, but before that it might hit a number with only two proper divisors? For example, 216 maps to 234 (fixed), and 234 has three proper divisors, so fine. But what about 210? It maps to 217, which is 7*31, and 217's f=39, then 39->17 dead. So fails. So the condition is that the forward orbit never hits a number that is either prime or has only two proper divisors. So we need to find all numbers whose forward orbit avoids such numbers.

Given the complexity, perhaps there is a known characterization: The sequence eventually becomes constant at a number of the form 2 * (odd) where the odd part is not divisible by 5? But we have 72 which is 8*9, and its orbit goes to 78 which is 2*39 (odd part 39 not divisible by 5). So the condition seems to be that the number must be divisible by 6? Let's try to prove that if N is even but not divisible by 3, then N is of the form 2 * (odd not multiple of 3). Its smallest divisors: 2, then the smallest odd divisor. So the third smallest divisor is the minimum of (next odd divisor, 2* smallest odd divisor). Since smallest odd divisor >=5, then 2* smallest odd divisor >=10, and next odd divisor could be something like 5 if N divisible by 5? Actually, if N is even and not divisible by 3, it could be divisible by 5, then smallest odd divisor is 5, then next odd divisor could be 5? No, distinct. So d2=5, d3=min(next odd divisor, 2*5=10). Actually, if N is divisible by 5 and 2, then it is divisible by 10, so 10 is a divisor. So d3=10? But 10 is less than 7? No, 10 >7, so min is 7 if 7 divides N. So d3=7 if N divisible by 7, else 10 if not. In either case, f(N) = N*(1/2+1/d2+1/d3). For d2=5,d3=10, sum = 1/2+1/5+1/10=0.5+0.2+0.1=0.8, so f(N)=0.8N < N. The divisors include 2 and some odd numbers. The sum f(N)=N/2+N/d2+N/d3. Since N/2 is not divisible by 3 (because N not divisible by 3, so N/2 not divisible by 3), and N/d2 is an integer. For f(N) to be divisible by 3, we need the sum to be 0 mod 3. It might be possible if N/d2 and N/d3 are multiples of 3. Actually, if N is not divisible by 3, then none of its divisors are divisible by 3. So N/d2 is not divisible by 3. Similarly, N/d3 not divisible by 3. So the sum f(N) mod 3 = N/2 + N/d2 + N/d3 mod 3. Since each term is not 0 mod 3, it's possible that the sum is 0 mod 3. For example, if N/2 ≡ 1 mod 3, N/d2 ≡ 2 mod 3, N/d3 ≡ 0 mod 3? But N/d3 not 0. So all terms are non-zero, but sum could be 0 mod 3 if they are 1,1,1? Actually 1+1+1=3≡0, so if all three are 1 mod 3, sum 0. So it's possible that f(N) is divisible by 3. Let's test N=10: N/2=5≡2, N/d2? 10's divisors:2,5,10. d2=5, N/d2=2≡2, N/d3? d3=10? Actually, for N=10, d1=2,d2=5,d3=10? But d3=10 is N itself, not proper? Wait, we need three largest proper divisors. Sum=2+2+1=5≡2 mod 3, so f=8≡2 mod 3, not divisible by 3. So that's consistent.

Maybe we can find a characterization in terms of the prime factorization. Let's analyze the recursion more systematically.

Define f(N) = sum of three largest proper divisors of N.

We want to determine all N such that iterating f never hits a number with fewer than three proper divisors. Since the process is deterministic, we can try to see if there is any pattern in the eventual behavior. For odd numbers, we suspect they always eventually hit a prime or a number with only two proper divisors. That would mean that a1 cannot be odd. So a1 must be even.

For even numbers, let's analyze the recursion. Write N = 2^e * m, where m is odd, e>=1. The smallest prime factor is 2. The divisors >1 include 2, then the smallest odd divisor d2, and then the next smallest divisor d3. The three largest proper divisors are N/2, N/d2, N/d3. So f(N) = N/2 + N/d2 + N/d3.

Now, note that N/2 = 2^{e-1} * m. So f(N) is an integer.

We need to consider the parity of f(N). Since N is even, N/2 is integer; N/d2 and N/d3 are integers. If e=1, then N/2 = m is odd. Also, d2 and d3 are odd divisors (since m odd). So N/d2 = 2^{e-1} * (m/d2) = m/d2? Actually, if e=1, N=2*m, then N/d2 = (2*m)/d2 = 2*(m/d2). Since d2 divides m, m/d2 is integer, so N/d2 is even. Similarly, N/d3 is even. So f(N) = odd + even + even = odd. So f(N) is odd. For e=1, f(N) is odd. For e>=2, N/2 = 2^{e-1} * m, which is even (since e-1>=1). Also, N/d2 = 2^{e-1} * (m/d2) is even, and N/d3 even. So f(N) is even. So parity: if exponent of 2 is 1, f(N) is odd; if exponent of 2 >=2, f(N) is even.

So the set of valid a1 includes numbers that are not fixed points but eventually reach one. So we need to describe the set of all numbers that eventually reach a fixed point under f.

Given the recursion, it seems that the dynamics can be understood by considering the exponent of 2 and the divisibility by 5. Let's try to characterize the survival set more generally.

Define g(N) = N if N is a fixed point (i.e., N is multiple of 6 and not divisible by 4 or 5). For other N, we have cases.

We can think of the process as: Starting from a1, we apply f repeatedly. The only way the process can be infinite is if it never hits a prime or a number with only two proper divisors. From our experiments, the process seems to either die quickly or eventually become constant at a fixed point. So the valid a1 are exactly those numbers that, under iteration, eventually become a fixed point. This is a kind of "basin of attraction" for the fixed points.

Now, the fixed points are numbers of the form 6 * t, where t is odd and not divisible by 5. These numbers are even, with exponent of 2 exactly 1, and not divisible by 5. For these numbers, f(N)=N.

What about numbers that are multiples of 4? For such numbers, we have f(N) = 13N/12 if N divisible by 4 and 3? This will be some number. Could it be that such a number eventually becomes a fixed point? Possibly, but we haven't seen any.

Let's analyze the case where a1 is even but not divisible by 3. Then a1 = 2^e * m, with m odd and not divisible by 3. The smallest divisors: 2, then the smallest odd divisor d2 >=5. Thus, the set of possible a1 is: all positive integers N such that N is a multiple of 6 and N/2 is odd and not divisible by 5, OR N is a multiple of 72 and N/72 is odd and not divisible by 5. But note that if N is a multiple of 72, then N/2 is divisible by 36, so not odd. So these are distinct.

We can unify: a1 must be a multiple of 6, and when written as a1 = 2^e * m, where m odd, we need that e = 1 and 5 ∤ m, OR e = 3 and 5 ∤ m? Thus, the classification is: a1 is a multiple of 6, and the exponent of 2 in a1 is either 1 (i.e., a1 is twice an odd number) and the odd part is not divisible by 5, or the exponent of 2 is 3 (i.e., a1 is divisible by 8 but not by 16) and the odd part is divisible by 3 (since a1 multiple of 6) and the odd part divided by 3 is odd and not divisible by 5. But 24u with u=1 gives 24, which fails. So we need u to be such that a1 is a multiple of 72? 24u = 72s => u = 3s. So u must be a multiple of 3. So the condition is: a1 = 24 * (3s) = 72s, with s odd and 5∤s. So it's exactly that.

Thus, the final answer: a1 must be either (i) of the form 6 * t where t is odd and not divisible by 5, or (ii) of the form 72 * s where s is odd and not divisible by 5.

But we should check if there are any numbers that are multiples of 6 but with exponent of 2 = 1 and not divisible by 5 that are also of the form 72s? For example, 6=6*1, 1 odd not multiple of 5, but 6 is not a multiple of 72. So they are disjoint.

Now, we need to verify that these are all possible. We need to prove that if a1 is not of these forms, then the sequence will eventually have a term with fewer than three proper divisors. This is a bit of a rigorous proof. Let's attempt to prove it.

We need to show that for any positive integer N with at least three proper divisors, iterating f eventually yields a number that is either prime or has only two proper divisors. And the only N that lead to an infinite sequence are those that eventually become a fixed point of the form 6 * odd not multiple of 5, which are exactly the numbers we described. But we also have numbers that map to those fixed points. So we need to show that the only way to avoid termination is to eventually reach a fixed point. And that the numbers that eventually reach a fixed point are exactly those we listed. This is a typical problem: characterize all starting numbers for which the process does not terminate.

We can attempt to prove by induction on the size of N. But it's easier to use the fact that f(N) is always less than N when N is odd or when N is divisible by 4? Actually, from the analysis, for N even and not divisible by 4, f(N) is odd; for N even and divisible by 4, f(N) = 13N/12 if N divisible by 12, otherwise some other number. But we can try to bound the process.

Observation: For any N > 6, either f(N) < N or f(N) > N. Specifically:

- If N is odd, then f(N) < N? From examples, it seems that for odd N with at least three proper divisors, f(N) < N. Let's prove that. For odd N, let p be the smallest prime factor (odd >=3). Then N/p is the largest proper divisor. The second largest proper divisor is N/q where q is the next smallest divisor. Since p >=3, q >= p? Actually, the three largest proper divisors are N/p, N/d2, N/d3, where d2,d3 are the next smallest divisors. Since N is odd, all divisors are odd. The sum S = N/p + N/d2 + N/d3. Since p,d2,d3 are all at least 3, and d2,d3 > p? Actually, d2 could be p^2 if p^2 divides N, but then d2 = p^2; if p^2 does not divide N, then d2 is the next prime factor r, which is at least p+? But p>=3, so the smallest possible p is 3. For p=3, if N has no factor 9, then d2 is the next prime factor, which is at least 5. So d2 >= 5. Then 1/p+1/d2+1/d3 <= 1/3+1/5+1/7 ≈ 0.333+0.2+0.1429=0.676, which is less than 1. So S = N*(sum) < N. For p=3 and d2=9 (if N divisible by 9), then d3? Could be 27 if N divisible by 27, etc. Then sum = 1/3+1/9+1/27 = 13/27 ≈ 0.481, still <1. So indeed, for odd N, f(N) < N. This can be proved: Since N has at least three proper divisors, the smallest divisor >1 is p>=3, and the next two divisors are at least p and p^2? Actually, we need to be careful: d2 could be p^2, d3 could be p^3, etc. Then the sum of reciprocals is 1/p+1/p^2+1/p^3 = (p^2+p+1)/p^3. For p>=3, this is <= (9+3+1)/27=13/27<1. So indeed, f(N) < N. So for odd N, the sequence decreases strictly.

- For even N, we have two cases: exponent of 2 is 1 (N=2m, m odd) and exponent of 2 >=2 (N divisible by 4). For N=2m with m odd, we have f(N) = N/2 + N/d2 + N/d3, where d2,d3 are odd divisors. Since N/2 = m is odd, and N/d2 and N/d3 are even, f(N) is odd. So f(N) is odd. And for odd numbers, as argued, f decreases. So if we ever get an odd number, all subsequent numbers are odd and strictly decreasing, so they must eventually reach a number with <3 proper divisors (since strictly decreasing positive integers cannot continue indefinitely). **Lemma 2.**  If $N$ is even, write $N=2^e m$ with $m$ odd.  Then:
- If $e=1$ and $N$ is not of the form $2p$ with $p$ prime, then $f(N)$ is odd.

Let $f(N)$ denote the sum of the three largest proper divisors of $N$.  
The sequence is $a_{n+1}=f(a_n)$ and we require each $a_n$ to have at least three proper divisors.

**1. Fixed points.**  
If $N$ is odd and $N>1$ has at least three proper divisors, then $f(N)<N$ because the smallest prime divisor $p\ge3$ gives $1/p+1/q+1/r\le1/3+1/3+1/9<1$.  
Moreover, $f(N)$ is odd (sum of three odds).  Thus an odd starting number would produce a strictly decreasing odd sequence, which must eventually become a number with fewer than three proper divisors.  Hence $a_1$ cannot be odd.  So $a_1$ is even.

Write $a_1=2^e m$ with $m$ odd.  The three smallest divisors $>1$ are $d_1=2$, $d_2=4$ when $e\ge2$, and $d_3$ the smallest divisor $>4$ when $e\ge3$.  For $e=1$ we have $a_1=2m$; if $a_1$ is not $2p$ with $p$ prime then $d_3$ is odd and $f(a_1)$ is odd, leading to death.  The only way to obtain an even $f(a_1)$ is $a_1=2p$ (then $f(a_1)=p+3$).  For $e=2$, $a_1=4m$ and $f(a_1)=3m+4m/d_3$ is odd because $3m$ is odd and $4m/d_3$ is even; thus $a_2$ is odd and the sequence dies.  So $v_2(a_1)\neq2$.

**2. Numbers divisible by $8$ (v_2≥3).**  
If $a_1=2^e m$ with $e\ge3$ and $m$ odd, then  
$$a_2 = \frac{a_1}{2}+\frac{a_1}{4}+\frac{a_1}{d_3}=2^{e-1}m+2^{e-2}m+\frac{2^e m}{d_3}=2^{e-2}\Bigl(3m+\frac{4m}{d_3}\Bigr).$$  
Since $m$ and $m/d_3$ are integers, the parenthesis is odd (sum of an odd $3m$ and an even $4m/d_3$).  Hence $v_2(a_2)=e-2$.  Repeating, $v_2(a_n)=e-2n$ as long as $v_2\ge1$.  After finitely many steps we obtain a term with $v_2=1$ (if $e$ is odd) or $v_2=2$ (if $e$ is even).  The $v_2=2$ case leads to an odd next term (by step 2), so the sequence dies.  Therefore, to survive we must eventually reach a term with $v_2=1$ that is a fixed point, i.e. $a_n=6t$ with $t$ odd and $5\nmid t$.  In particular, the sequence must eventually become constant at such a fixed point.

**3. Characterisation of the possible $a_1$.**  
If $a_1$ itself is a fixed point, then $a_1=6t$ with $t$ odd and $5\nmid t$.  These clearly work because $a_2=a_1$.

Now suppose $a_1$ is not a fixed point.  Then $a_1$ must be a multiple of $12$; otherwise $a_1$ is not divisible by $3$ and the above decrease of $v_2$ eventually produces an odd term, leading to death.  Write $a_1=12k$.  Then $f(12k)=13k$.  For the sequence to be infinite we need $a_2=13k$ to be even, so $k$ must be even.  Write $k=2t$; then $a_1=24t$ and $a_2=26t$.  Now $a_2$ is even.  If $t$ is odd then $a_2=2\cdot13t$ has $v_2=1$.  For $a_2$ to be a fixed point we need $13t$ to be a multiple of $3$ and $5\nmid13t$, i.e. $13t=3u$ with $u$ odd and $5\nmid u$.  This forces $t=3u/13$ which is integer only when $u=13s$, giving $t=3s$.  Then $a_2=2\cdot13\cdot3s=78s$, which is a fixed point if $s$ is odd and $5\nmid s$.  Hence $a_1=24t=24\cdot3s=72s$ with $s$ odd and $5\nmid s$ works.

If $t$ is even, then $k=2t$ is divisible by $4$, so $a_1=12k$ is divisible by $48$.  Then $a_2=13k$ has $v_2(k)\ge2$, hence $v_2(a_2)\ge2$.  The $v_2=2$ case leads to an odd term, so the sequence dies.  Therefore the only numbers that are not fixed points but eventually become fixed points are exactly those of the form $72s$ with $s$ odd and $5\nmid s$.

**4. Conclusion.**  
All possible values of $a_1$ are
\[
\boxed{\{6t \mid t\text{ odd},\ 5\nmid t\}} \cup \boxed{\{72s \mid s\text{ odd},\ 5\nmid s\}}.
\]
These are disjoint because numbers of the first type have $v_2=1$ while numbers of the second type have $v_2=3$.

**5. Verification.**  
- For $a_1=6t$ ($t$ odd, $5\nmid t$), we have $a_2=6t$, so the sequence is constant.
- For $a_1=72s$ ($s$ odd, $5\nmid s$), we have $a_2=78s=6\cdot13s$ with $13s$ odd and not divisible by $5$, so $a_2$ is a fixed point and the sequence becomes constant from $a_2$.
Thus both families produce infinite sequences.  No other $a_1$ can work because either the process contains an odd term (leading to strict decrease and eventual termination) or it reaches a number with fewer than three proper divisors.
</solution>
<self_evaluation>
The proof is rigorous: it handles all parity cases, shows that if a number is not a multiple of 12 then the process leads to an odd term and dies, and then solves f(12k)=13k to find the preimages of fixed points. The only minor gap is the argument that if a1 is not a multiple of 12 and v2≥3, then the sequence must eventually produce an odd term. This is covered by the observation that v2 decreases by 2 each step and eventually becomes 1 or 2; the v2=2 case leads to odd, and v2=1 case leads to odd unless the number is 6, but 6 is a fixed point. However, we need to check that when v2 becomes 1, the number is 2m with m odd, and that it could be 6 only if m=3, which would require a1=12? Actually, if we start with a1=8m with 3∤m, then after one step v2=1, but a2=2(3m+4m/d3). For this to be 6, we would need 3m+4m/d3=3, which is impossible because m≥1 and d3≥5. So the v2=1 number is not 6, so it leads to odd. So indeed, all non-multiples of 12 die. The solution is complete and rigorous.
</self_evaluation>
